      <p>
       <b>
        Sicilian
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="scn" xml:lang="scn">
         <i>
          sicilianu
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ; in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Siciliano
       </i>
       ; also known as
       <b>
        Siculo
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="scn" xml:lang="scn">
         <i>
          siculu
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) or
       <b>
        Calabro-Sicilian
       </b>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       spoken on the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       and its satellite islands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also spoken in southern
       <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
        Calabria
       </a>
       (where it is called
       <b>
        Southern Calabro
       </b>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       specifically in the province of
       <a href="/wiki/Reggio_Calabria" title="Reggio Calabria">
        Reggio Calabria
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varvaro_A._1988_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Varvaro_A._1988-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whose dialect is viewed as being part of the continuum of the Sicilian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Central Calabria, the southern parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">
        Apulia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Salento" title="Salento">
        Salento
       </a>
       (where it is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Salentino_dialect" title="Salentino dialect">
        Salentino
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">
        Campania
       </a>
       , on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">
        Italian peninsula
       </a>
       , where it is called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilentano_dialect" title="Cilentano dialect">
        Cilentano
       </a>
       are viewed as being part of the broader Far Southern Italian language group (in Italian:
       <i>
        Italiano meridionale estremo
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
         Ethnologue
        </a>
       </i>
       (see
       <a href="#Ethnologue_report_on_Sicilian">
        below
       </a>
       for more detail) describes Sicilian as being "distinct enough from
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Standard Italian
       </a>
       to be considered a separate language"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is recognized as a "minority language" by
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sicilian has the oldest literary tradition of the modern Italian languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Status">
        Status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Sicilian is currently spoken by the majority of the inhabitants of Sicily and by emigrant populations around the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The latter are found in the countries which attracted large numbers of Sicilian immigrants during the course of the past century or so, especially the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       . In the past four or five decades, large numbers of Sicilians were also attracted to the industrial zones of
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">
        northern Italy
       </a>
       and areas of the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       , especially
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is not used as an official language anywhere, even within Sicily. Currently the government does not regulate the language in any way. However, in recent years the non-profit organisation
       <a href="/wiki/Cad%C3%A8mia_Siciliana" title="Cadèmia Siciliana">
        Cadèmia Siciliana
       </a>
       has created an orthographic proposal to help normalise the written form of the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, since its inception in 1951, the
       <a href="/wiki/Centro_di_studi_filologici_e_linguistici_siciliani" title="Centro di studi filologici e linguistici siciliani">
        Centro di studi filologici e linguistici siciliani
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">
        Palermo
       </a>
       has been researching and publishing descriptive information on the Sicilian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The autonomous regional parliament of Sicily has legislated Regional Law No 9/2011 to encourage the teaching of Sicilian at all schools, but inroads into the education system have been slow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Centro_di_studi_filologici_e_linguistici_siciliani" title="Centro di studi filologici e linguistici siciliani">
        CSFLS
       </a>
       has created a textbook "Dialektos" to comply with the law however it does not provide an orthography to write the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although within Sicily it is only taught as part of
       <a href="/wiki/Dialectology" title="Dialectology">
        dialectology
       </a>
       courses, outside of Italy, Sicilian language has been taught at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">
        University of Pennsylvania
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Universit%C3%A9_de_la_Manouba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Université de la Manouba (page does not exist)">
        Université de la Manouba
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The language is officially recognized in the municipal statutes of Sicilian towns, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Caltagirone" title="Caltagirone">
        Caltagirone
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammichele" title="Grammichele">
        Grammichele
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in which the "inalienable historical and cultural value of the Sicilian language" is proclaimed. Further, the Sicilian language would be protected and promoted under the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       (ECRML). However, Italy has signed this treaty, but the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Parliament" title="Italian Parliament">
        Italian Parliament
       </a>
       has not ratified it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is not included in Italian Law no. 482/1999, although some other minority languages of Sicily are.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Sicilan language is spoken in various Sicilian American communities in both the United States and Canada (especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">
        Montreal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">
        Toronto
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">
        Hamilton
       </a>
       ), and is preserved and taught through family association, church organizations and societies, as well as social and ethnic historical clubs, and even in Internet social groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnologue_report_on_Sicilian">
        Ethnologue report on Sicilian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ethnologue report on Sicilian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romance-lg-classification-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="1011" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/375px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/563px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/750px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png 2x" width="375"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romance-lg-classification-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Chart of Romance languages based on structural and comparative criteria (not on socio-functional ones). Koryakov (2001), shows the relationship of the three main sub-groupings in the "wider Sicilian" language cluster, and also the various relationships between other romance languages which have influenced the development of Sicilian
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koryakov_33-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Koryakov-33">
           [33]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_names">
        Other names
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Other names">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Alternative names of Sicilian are Calabro-Sicilian, Sicilianu, and Siculu.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term Calabro-Sicilian refers to the fact that a form of Sicilian, is spoken in southern Calabria, in particular, in the province of Reggio Calabria.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Sicilianu
       </i>
       is the name of the language in Sicily itself.
      </p>
      <p>
       The term "Siculu" describes one of the larger prehistoric groups living in Sicily (the
       <a href="/wiki/Sicels" title="Sicels">
        Sicels
       </a>
       or Siculi) before the arrival of
       <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
        Greeks
       </a>
       in the 8th century BC
       <i>
        (see below)
       </i>
       . It can also be used as an adjective to qualify, or further elaborate on, the origins of a person, for example: Siculo-American (
       <i>
        siculu-miricanu
       </i>
       ) or Siculo-Australian.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As a language, Sicilian has its own
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialects
       </a>
       , in the following main groupings:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_3-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Western Sicilian (in
        <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">
         Palermo
        </a>
        as Palermitano,
        <a href="/wiki/Trapani" title="Trapani">
         Trapani
        </a>
        as Trapanese, Central-Western
        <a href="/wiki/Agrigento" title="Agrigento">
         Agrigentino
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Central Metafonetica (in the central part of Sicily that includes some areas of the Provinces of
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Caltanissetta" title="Province of Caltanissetta">
         Caltanissetta
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Messina" title="Province of Messina">
         Messina
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Enna" title="Province of Enna">
         Enna
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Palermo" title="Province of Palermo">
         Palermo
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Agrigento" title="Province of Agrigento">
         Agrigento
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Southeast Metafonetica (in the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Ragusa" title="Province of Ragusa">
         Province of Ragusa
        </a>
        and the adjoining area within the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Syracuse" title="Province of Syracuse">
         Province of Syracuse
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Ennese (in the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Enna" title="Province of Enna">
         province of Enna
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Eastern Nonmetafonetica (in the area including the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Catania" title="Province of Catania">
         province of Catania
        </a>
        as Catanese, the second largest city in Sicily, and the adjoining area within the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Syracuse" title="Province of Syracuse">
         Province of Syracuse
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Messinese (in the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Messina" title="Province of Messina">
         province of Messina
        </a>
        , the third largest city in Sicily)
       </li>
       <li>
        Isole Eolie (in the
        <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_Islands" title="Aeolian Islands">
         Aeolian Islands
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Pantesco (on the island of
        <a href="/wiki/Pantelleria" title="Pantelleria">
         Pantelleria
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Reggino (in the
        <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_City_of_Reggio_Calabria" title="Metropolitan City of Reggio Calabria">
         Metropolitan City of Reggio Calabria
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-35">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varvaro_A._1988_5-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Varvaro_A._1988-5">
          [5]
         </a>
        </sup>
        especially on the
        <a href="/wiki/Scilla,_Calabria" title="Scilla, Calabria">
         Scilla
        </a>
        -
        <a href="/wiki/Bova,_Calabria" title="Bova, Calabria">
         Bova
        </a>
        line,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
         <a href="#cite_note-36">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and excluding the areas of
        <a href="/wiki/Locri" title="Locri">
         Locri
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Rosarno" title="Rosarno">
         Rosarno
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avolio_F._2009_37-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Avolio_F._2009-37">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
        which represent the first isogloss which divide Sicilian from the continental varieties
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avolio_F._2009_37-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Avolio_F._2009-37">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lingua_siciliana.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="811" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Lingua_siciliana.png/220px-Lingua_siciliana.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Lingua_siciliana.png/330px-Lingua_siciliana.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Lingua_siciliana.png/440px-Lingua_siciliana.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_influences">
        Early influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Because
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       is the largest island in the
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
        Mediterranean Sea
       </a>
       and many peoples have passed through it (
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
        Phoenicians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        Ancient Greeks
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
        Romans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">
        Ostrogoths
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine Greeks
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">
        Moors
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">
        Normans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swabians" title="Swabians">
        Swabians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">
        Spaniards
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Austrians" title="Austrians">
        Austrians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
        Italians
       </a>
       ), Sicilian displays the rich and varied influence of several languages on its lexical stock and grammar. Such languages include
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
        Norman
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lombard_language" title="Lombard language">
        Lombard
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , and the influence from the island's pre-Indo-European inhabitants. The very earliest influences, visible in Sicilian to this day, exhibit both prehistoric Mediterranean elements and prehistoric
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       elements, and occasionally a blending of both.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Before the
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilia_(Roman_province)" title="Sicilia (Roman province)">
        Roman conquest
       </a>
       (3rd century BC), Sicily was occupied by various populations. The earliest of these populations were the
       <a href="/wiki/Sicani" title="Sicani">
        Sicani
       </a>
       , considered to be authoctonous.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Sicels" title="Sicels">
        Sicels
       </a>
       (or Siculi or Siculians) arrived between the second and first millennia BC), and
       <a href="/wiki/Elymians" title="Elymians">
        Elymians
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Morgetes" title="Morgetes">
        Morgetes
       </a>
       arrived during this same period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These populations in turn were followed by Phoenicians (between the 10th and 8th centuries BC)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        Greeks
       </a>
       (from the 8th century BC).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Greek-language influence remains strongly visible, while the influences from the other groups are less obvious.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       What can be stated with certainty is that there remain pre-Indo-European words in Sicilian of an ancient Mediterranean origin, but one cannot be more precise than that.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of the three main prehistoric groups, only the Sicels were known to be Indo-European with a degree of certainty, and their speech is likely to have been closely related to that of the Romans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino_41-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stratification">
        Stratification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stratification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The following table, listing words for "twins", illustrates the difficulty linguists face in tackling the various sub-strata of the Sicilian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th scope="col">
         Stratum
        </th>
        <th scope="col">
         Word
        </th>
        <th colspan="2" scope="col">
         Source
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Modern
        </td>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         giameddi
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
          Italian
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          gemelli
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td rowspan="2">
         Medieval
        </td>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         bizzuni, vuzzuni
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
          Norman
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          bessons
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         binelli
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Ligurian_(Romance_language)" title="Ligurian (Romance language)">
          Ligurian
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          beneli
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td rowspan="4">
         Ancient
        </td>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         èmmuli
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
          Latin
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          gemelli
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         cucchi
        </th>
        <td>
         Latin
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          copula
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         minzuddi
        </th>
        <td>
         Latin
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          medii
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="font-weight: normal; font-style: italic;">
         ièmiddi, ièddimi
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
          Ancient Greek
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         δίδυμοι
         <i>
          dídymoi
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       A similar qualifier can be applied to many of the words that appear in this article. Sometimes we may know that a particular word has a prehistoric derivation, but we do not know whether the Sicilians have inherited it directly from the indigenous populations, or whether it has come to them via another route. Similarly, we might know that a particular word has a Greek origin but we do not know from which Greek period the Sicilians first used it (pre-Roman occupation or during its
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine period
       </a>
       ), or once again, whether the particular word may even have come to Sicily via another route. For instance, by the time the Romans had occupied Sicily during the 3rd century BC, the Latin language had made its own borrowings from Greek.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-classical_period">
        Pre-classical period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pre-classical period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The words with a prehistoric Mediterranean derivation often refer to plants native to the Mediterranean region or to other natural features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino10_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino10-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Bearing in mind the qualifiers mentioned above (alternative sources are provided where known), examples of such words include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         alastra
        </i>
        (a thorny, prickly plant native to the Mediterranean region; but also Greek
        <i>
         kèlastron
        </i>
        and may in fact have penetrated Sicilian via one of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaulish_languages" title="Gaulish languages">
         Gaulish languages
        </a>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino10_44-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino10-44">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         ammarrari
        </i>
        "to dam or block a canal or running water" (but also Spanish
        <i>
         embarrar
        </i>
        – to muddy)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         calancuni
        </i>
        "ripples caused by a fast running river"
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         calanna
        </i>
        "landslide of rocks"
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         racioppu
        </i>
        "stalk or stem, e.g. of a fruit" (ancient Mediterranean word
        <i>
         rak
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         timpa
        </i>
        "crag, cliff" (Greek
        <i>
         tymba
        </i>
        , Latin
        <i>
         tumba
        </i>
        and Catalan
        <i>
         timba
        </i>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       There are also Sicilian words with an ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       origin that do not appear to have come to the language via any of the major language groups normally associated with Sicilian, i.e. they have been independently derived from a very early Indo-European source. The Sicels are a possible source of such words, but there is also the possibility of a cross-over between ancient Mediterranean words and introduced Indo-European forms. Some examples of Sicilian words with an ancient Indo-European origin:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         dudda
        </i>
        "mulberry" (similar to Indo-European
        <i>
         *roudho
        </i>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rhudd" title="wikt:rhudd">
          rhudd
         </a>
        </i>
        "red, crimson")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         scrozzu
        </i>
        "not well developed" (similar to
        <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
         Lithuanian
        </a>
        <i>
         su-skurdes
        </i>
        with a similar meaning and
        <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
         Old High German
        </a>
        <i>
         scurz
        </i>
        "short")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         sfunnacata
        </i>
        "multitude, vast number" (from Indo-European
        <i>
         *und
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         *fund
        </i>
        "water".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_influences">
        Greek influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Greek influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The following Sicilian words are of a Greek origin (including some examples where it is unclear whether the word is derived directly from Greek, or via Latin):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         babbiari
        </i>
        – "to fool around" (from
        <i>
         babazo
        </i>
        , which also gives the Sicilian words:
        <i>
         babbazzu
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         babbu
        </i>
        – stupid; but Latin
        <i>
         babulus
        </i>
        and Spanish
        <i>
         babieca
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         bucali
        </i>
        – "pitcher" (from
        <i>
         baukalion
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-8">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         bùmmulu
        </i>
        – "water receptacle" (from
        <i>
         bombylos
        </i>
        ; but Latin
        <i>
         bombyla
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino12_46-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino12-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cartedda
        </i>
        – "basket" (from
        <i>
         kártallos
        </i>
        ; but Latin
        <i>
         cartellum
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino12_46-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino12-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         carusu
        </i>
        – "boy" (from
        <i>
         kouros
        </i>
        ; but Latin
        <i>
         carus
        </i>
        – dear,
        <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
         Sanskrit
        </a>
        <i>
         caruh
        </i>
        – amiable)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-9">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         casèntaru
        </i>
        – "earthworm" (from
        <i>
         gâs ènteron
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-10">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cirasa
        </i>
        – "cherry" (from
        <i>
         kerasos
        </i>
        ; but Latin
        <i>
         cerasum
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-11">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cona
        </i>

        <i>

         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>

       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Germanic_influences">
        Germanic influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Germanic influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       From 476 to 535, the
       <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">
        Ostrogoths
       </a>
       ruled Sicily, although their presence apparently did not impact the Sicilian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The few
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       influences to be found in Sicilian do not appear to originate from this period. One exception might be
       <i>
        abbanniari
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        vanniari
       </i>
       "to hawk goods, proclaim publicly", from
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       <i>
        bandujan
       </i>
       – to give a signal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also possible is
       <i>
        schimmenti
       </i>
       "diagonal" from Gothic
       <i>
        slimbs
       </i>
       "slanting".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other sources of Germanic influences include the
       <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">
        Hohenstaufen
       </a>
       rule of the 13th century, words of
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       origin contained within the speech of 11th century
       <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">
        Normans
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">
        Lombard
       </a>
       settlers, and the short period of
       <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">
        Austrian
       </a>
       rule in the 18th century.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_influence">
        Arabic influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Arabic influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 535,
       <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">
        Justinian I
       </a>
       made Sicily a
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine
       </a>
       province, which returned the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek language
       </a>
       to a position of prestige, at least on an official level.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At this point in time the island can be considered a border zone with high levels of bilingualism. Latinisation was mostly concentrated in western Sicily,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas Eastern Sicily remained predominantly Greek. As the power of the Byzantine Empire waned,
       <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">
        Sicily was progressively conquered
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">
        Saracens
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
        North Africa
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">
         Ifriqiya
        </a>
       </i>
       ), from the mid 9th to mid 10th centuries, and remained there long enough to develop a distinctive local variety of Arabic,
       <a href="/wiki/Siculo-Arabic" title="Siculo-Arabic">
        Siculo-Arabic
       </a>
       (at present extinct in Sicily but surviving in the
       <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">
        Maltese language
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its influence is noticeable in around 300 Sicilian words, most of which relate to agriculture and related activities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is understandable because of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">
        Arab Agricultural Revolution
       </a>
       ; the
       <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">
        Saracens
       </a>
       introduced to
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       their advanced irrigation and farming techniques and a new range of crops, nearly all of which remain endemic to the island to this day.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some words of
       <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_Arabic_on_other_languages" title="Influence of Arabic on other languages">
        Arabic origin
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         azzizzari
        </i>
        – "to embellish" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         عزيز
        </span>
        <i>
         ʿazīz
        </i>
        "precious, beautiful")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-19">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         babbaluciu
        </i>
        – "snail" (from
        <i>
         babus
        </i>
        ; but Greek
        <i>
         βουβαλάκιον
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-20">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         burnia
        </i>
        – "jar" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         برنية
        </span>
        <i>
         burniya
        </i>
        ; but Latin
        <i>
         hirnea
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-21">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cafisu
        </i>
        – "measure for liquids" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         قافز
        </span>
        <i>
         qāfiz
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cassata" title="Cassata">
          cassata
         </a>
        </i>
        – "Sicilian
        <a href="/wiki/Ricotta" title="Ricotta">
         ricotta
        </a>
        cake" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         قشطة
        </span>
        <i>
         qishṭah
        </i>
        , chiefly North African; but Latin
        <i>
         caseata
        </i>
        – something made from cheese)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-23">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         gèbbia
        </i>
        – artificial pond to store water for irrigation (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         جابية
        </span>
        <i>
         jābiya
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         giuggiulena
        </i>
        – "sesame seed" (
        <i>
         giulgiulan
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-25">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Mafia" title="Mafia">
          mafia
         </a>
        </i>
        – "
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/swagger" title="wikt:swagger">
         swagger
        </a>
        , boldness/bravado" (from
        <i>
         mahyas
        </i>
        "aggressive boasting, bragging", or from مرفود
        <i>
         marfud
        </i>
        "rejected")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mafiusu_50-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-mafiusu-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         ràisi
        </i>
        – "leader" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         ريس
        </span>
        <i>
         raʾīs
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-26">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         saia
        </i>
        – "canal" (from
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         ساقية
        </span>
        <i>
         sāqiya
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-27">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         zaffarana
        </i>
        – "saffron, type of plant whose flowers are used for medicinal purposes and in Sicilian cooking" (from
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         زعفران
        </span>
        <i>
         zafrān
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         zagara
        </i>
        – "blossom" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         زهرة
        </span>
        <i>
         zahrah
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-28">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         zibbibbu
        </i>
        – "type of dried grape" (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         زبيب
        </span>
        <i>
         zabīb
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         zuccu
        </i>
        – "market" (from
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         سوق
        </span>
        <i>
         sūq
        </i>
        ; but
        <a href="/wiki/Aragonese_language" title="Aragonese language">
         Aragonese
        </a>
        <i>
         soccu
        </i>
        and Spanish
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/zoque" title="wikt:zoque">
          zoque
         </a>
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Bibbirria, the northern gate of
        <a href="/wiki/Agrigento" title="Agrigento">
         Agrigento
        </a>
        (
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         باب الرياح
        </span>
        <i>
         bāb al-riyāḥ
        </i>
        "Gate of the Winds").
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Throughout the Islamic epoch of Sicilian history, a significant Greek-speaking population remained on the island and continued to use the Greek language, or most certainly a variant of Greek influenced by Arabic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       What is less clear is the extent to which a Latin-speaking population survived on the island. While a form of Vulgar Latin clearly survived in isolated communities during the Islamic epoch, there is much debate as to the influence it had (if any) on the development of the Sicilian language, following the re-Latinisation of Sicily (discussed in the next section).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_developments_in_the_Middle_Ages">
        Linguistic developments in the Middle Ages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Linguistic developments in the Middle Ages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="550" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG/220px-Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG/330px-Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG/440px-Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scribes_01_Kingdom_of_Sicily_Petrus_de_Ebulo.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An 1196 miniature depicting the various
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scribes" title="Scribes">
          scribes
         </a>
         (1.
         <i>
          Greeks
         </i>
         2.
         <i>
          Saracens
         </i>
         3.
         <i>
          Latins
         </i>
         ) for the various populations of the
         <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">
          Kingdom of Sicily
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       By 1000 AD, the whole of what is today southern Italy, including Sicily, was a complex mix of small
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">
        states
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">
        principalities
       </a>
       , languages and religions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The whole of Sicily was controlled by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saracens" title="Saracens">
        Saracens
       </a>
       , at the elite level, but the general population remained a mix of Muslims and Greek or Siculo-Arabic speaking Orthodox Christians. There were also a component of immigrants from North Africa (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">
         Ifriqiya
        </a>
       </i>
       ). The far south of the Italian peninsula was part of the Byzantine empire although many communities were reasonably independent of
       <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">
        Constantinople
       </a>
       . The principality of Salerno was controlled by Lombards (or Langobards), who had also started to make some incursions into Byzantine territory and had managed to establish some isolated independent
       <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">
        city-states
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Norwich-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was into this climate that the Normans thrust themselves with increasing numbers during the first half of the 11th century.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_and_French_influence">
        Norman and French influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Norman and French influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       When the two most famous of
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">
        Southern Italy
       </a>
       's Norman adventurers,
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_I_of_Sicily" title="Roger I of Sicily">
        Roger of Hauteville
       </a>
       and his brother,
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Guiscard" title="Robert Guiscard">
        Robert Guiscard
       </a>
       , began their conquest of Sicily in 1061, they already controlled the far south of Italy (
       <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">
        Apulia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
        Calabria
       </a>
       ). It took Roger 30 years to complete the conquest of Sicily (Robert died in 1085).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Norwich-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the aftermath of the Norman conquest of Sicily, the revitalization of Latin in Sicily had begun, and a large number of Norman French words would be absorbed:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         accattari
        </i>
        – "to buy" (Norman French
        <i>
         acater
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         ammucciari
        </i>
        – to hide (Norman French
        <i>
         mucher
        </i>
        ; but Greek
        <i>
         mùkhos
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         bucceri
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         vucceri
        </i>
        ) – "butcher" (from OF
        <i>
         bouchier
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         custureri
        </i>
        – "tailor" (OF
        <i>
         cousturier
        </i>
        ; Modern
        <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
         French
        </a>
        <i>
         couturier
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         firranti
        </i>
        – "grey" (from Old French
        <i>
         ferrant
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-32">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         foḍḍi
        </i>
        – "mad" (OF
        <i>
         fol
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         giugnettu
        </i>
        – "July" (OF
        <i>
         juignet
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-8">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         ladiu
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         laiu
        </i>
        – "ugly" (
        <i>
         laid
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-9">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         largasìa
        </i>
        – "generosity" (
        <i>
         largesse
        </i>
        ; but also Spanish
        <i>
         largueza
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         puseri
        </i>
        – "thumb" (OF
        <i>
         pochier
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         racina
        </i>
        – "grape" (
        <i>
         raisin
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-10">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         raggia
        </i>
        – "anger" (
        <i>
         rage
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-11">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         trippari
        </i>
        – "to hop, skip" (Norman French
        <i>
         triper
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The following factors that emerged during or immediately after the conquest were to prove critical in the formation of the Sicilian language:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The Normans brought with them not only their own Norman-speaking kin (more than likely in quite small numbers) but also mercenaries from mainland Italy. In particular, they included Lombards with their
        <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_languages" title="Gallo-Italic languages">
         Gallo-Italic language
        </a>
        and other Italians from around
        <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">
         Campania
        </a>
        . The latter would bring with them the
        <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
         Vulgar Latin
        </a>
        from that region, a language not too different from that to be found in central
        <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
         Italy
        </a>
        at the time.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-12">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The thirty-year-long war of conquest and the eradication of Islam resulted in the depopulation of Saracens in most parts of Sicily, in particular, in Central Sicily.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-13">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Further migrations to settle the depopulated areas were encouraged from the mainland by Roger; in particular, Italian settlers from areas controlled by the
        <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
         Catholic Church
        </a>
        . The western parts of Sicily were colonised by migrants from Campania, and the central-eastern parts by settlers from the western
        <a href="/wiki/Po_Valley" title="Po Valley">
         Po Valley
        </a>
        in northern Italy, who also brought with them a Gallo-Italic language. After the death of Roger I and under the regency of
        <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_del_Vasto" title="Adelaide del Vasto">
         Adelaide del Vasto
        </a>
        during the minority of her son,
        <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">
         Roger II
        </a>
        (herself from Northern Italy), the process of Italian colonisation from mainland Italy was intensified.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-14">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich_52-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Norwich-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The main factors that go into framing modern Sicilian language can be seen. The Vulgar Latin base (predominantly from Campania) was similar to the Vulgar Latin in central Italy (and therefore, by implication, reasonably similar to the Vulgar Latin in Tuscany that would eventually form the base for the national language). This base from Campania was influenced by the many Gallic influences present in Sicily at the time, namely Norman, French and Langobardic. There were also remnants of Arabic and Greek that the new language eventually replaced, but hundreds of words remained in the vocabulary of the newly emerging Romance language.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Gallic_influences">
        Other Gallic influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other Gallic influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic" title="Gallo-Italic">
        Northern Italian
       </a>
       influence is of particular interest. Even to the present day,
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_of_Sicily" title="Gallo-Italic of Sicily">
        Gallo-Italic of Sicily
       </a>
       exists in the areas where the Northern Italian colonies were the strongest, namely
       <a href="/wiki/Novara_di_Sicilia" title="Novara di Sicilia">
        Novara
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nicosia,_Sicily" title="Nicosia, Sicily">
        Nicosia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sperlinga" title="Sperlinga">
        Sperlinga
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aidone" title="Aidone">
        Aidone
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Piazza_Armerina" title="Piazza Armerina">
        Piazza Armerina
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Siculo-Gallic dialect did not survive in other major Italian colonies, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Randazzo" title="Randazzo">
        Randazzo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bronte,_Sicily" title="Bronte, Sicily">
        Bronte
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Patern%C3%B2" title="Paternò">
        Paternò
       </a>
       (although they influenced the local Sicilian vernacular). The Gallo-Italic influence was also felt on the Sicilian language itself, as follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull_48-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hull-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         sòggiru
        </i>
        – father-in-law (from
        <i>
         suoxer
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cugnatu
        </i>
        – brother-in-law (from
        <i>
         cognau
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         figghiozzu
        </i>
        – godson (from
        <i>
         figlioz
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         orbu
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         orvu
        </i>
        – blind (from
        <i>
         orb
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         arricintari
        </i>
        – to rinse (from
        <i>
         rexentar
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         unni
        </i>
        – where (from
        <i>
         ond
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        the names of the days of the week:
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           luni
          </i>
          – Monday (from
          <i>
           lunes
          </i>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           marti
          </i>
          – Tuesday (from
          <i>
           martes
          </i>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           mèrcuri
          </i>
          – Wednesday (from
          <i>
           mèrcor
          </i>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           jovi
          </i>
          – Thursday (from
          <i>
           juovia
          </i>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           vènniri
          </i>
          – Friday (from
          <i>
           vènner
          </i>
          )
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The origins of another Romance influence, that of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Occitan" title="Old Occitan">
        Old Occitan
       </a>
       , had three possible sources:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        As mentioned above, the number of actual Normans in Sicily is unlikely to have ever been significant. They were boosted by mercenaries from southern Italy, but it is possible also that mercenaries came from as far away as southern France. The Normans made
        <a href="/wiki/San_Fratello" title="San Fratello">
         San Fratello
        </a>
        a garrison town in the early years of the occupation of the northeastern corner of Sicily. To this day (in ever decreasing numbers) a Siculo-Gallic dialect is spoken in San Fratello that is clearly influenced by Old Occitan, which leads to the conclusion that a significant number in the garrison came from that part of France.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Privitera_53-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Privitera-53">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This may well explain the dialect spoken only in San Fratello, but it does not wholly explain the diffusion of many Occitan words into the Sicilian language. On that point, there are two other possibilities:
       </li>
       <li>
        Some Occitan words may have entered the language during the regency of
        <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Navarre" title="Margaret of Navarre">
         Margaret of Navarre
        </a>
        between 1166 and 1171, when her son,
        <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_Sicily" title="William II of Sicily">
         William II of Sicily
        </a>
        , succeeded to the throne at the age of 12. Her closest advisers, entourage and administrators were from the south of France,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich_52-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Norwich-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and many Occitan words entered the language during this period.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_School" title="Sicilian School">
         Sicilian School
        </a>
        of poetry was strongly influenced by the Occitan of the
        <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">
         troubadour
        </a>
        tradition.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Privitera_53-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Privitera-53">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This element is deeply embedded in Sicilian culture: for example, the tradition of Sicilian puppetry (
        <i>
         opira dî puppi
        </i>
        ) and the tradition of the
        <i>
         cantastorii
        </i>
        (literally
        <i>
         sing stories
        </i>
        ). Occitan troubadours were active during the reign of
        <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">
         Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor
        </a>
        , and some Occitan words would have passed into the Sicilian language via this route.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Some examples of Sicilian words derived from Occitan:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         addumari
        </i>
        – to light, to turn something on (from
        <i>
         allumar
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-36">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         aggrifari
        </i>
        – to kidnap, abduct (from
        <i>
         grifar
        </i>
        ; but also German
        <i>
         greiffen
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-37">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         banna
        </i>
        – side, place (from
        <i>
         banda
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-38">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         burgisi
        </i>
        – landowners, citizens (from
        <i>
         borges
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         lascu
        </i>
        – sparse, thin, infrequent (from
        <i>
         lasc
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-39">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         paraggiu
        </i>
        – equal (from
        <i>
         paratge
        </i>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-40">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sicilian_School_of_Poetry">
        Sicilian School of Poetry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sicilian School of Poetry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_School" title="Sicilian School">
        Sicilian School
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       It was during the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">
        Frederick II
       </a>
       (or Frederick I of Sicily) between 1198 and 1250, with his patronage of the Sicilian School, that Sicilian became the first of the modern Italic languages to be used as a
       <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">
        literary language
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla141_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla141-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The influence of the school and the use of Sicilian itself as a poetic language was acknowledged by the two great Tuscan writers of the early Renaissance period,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">
        Dante
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">
        Petrarch
       </a>
       . The influence of the Sicilian language should not be underestimated in the eventual formulation of a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
       </i>
       that was to become modern
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       . The victory of the
       <a href="/wiki/Capetian_House_of_Anjou" title="Capetian House of Anjou">
        Angevin
       </a>
       army over the Sicilians at
       <a href="/wiki/Benevento" title="Benevento">
        Benevento
       </a>
       in 1266 not only marked the end of the 136-year Norman-
       <a href="/wiki/Swabians" title="Swabians">
        Swabian
       </a>
       reign in Sicily but also effectively ensured that the centre of literary influence would eventually move from Sicily to Tuscany.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla141_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla141-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While Sicilian, as both an official and a literary language, would continue to exist for another two centuries, the language would soon follow the fortunes of the kingdom itself in terms of prestige and influence.
      </p>
      <p>
       As a side note, there are some Germanic influences in the Sicilian language, and many of these date back to the time of the Swabian kings (amongst whom
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">
        Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor
       </a>
       enjoyed the longest reign). It should be noted that some of the words below are "reintroductions" of Latin words (also found in modern Italian) that had been Germanicized at some point (e.g. "Vastare" in Latin to
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "guastare" in modern Italian). Words that probably originate from this era include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         arbitriari
        </i>
        – to work in the fields (from
        <i>
         arbeit
        </i>
        ; but other possible Latin derivations)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-41">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         vardari
        </i>
        – to watch over (from
        <i>
         wardon
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         guddefi
        </i>
        – forest, woods (from
        <i>
         wald
        </i>
