        , note resemblance to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">
         Anglo-Saxon
        </a>
        <i>
         wudu
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-43">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         guzzuniari
        </i>
        – to wag, as in a tail (from
        <i>
         hutsen
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         lancedda
        </i>
        – terracotta jug for holding water (from Old High German
        <i>
         lagella
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         sparagnari
        </i>
        – to save money (from Old High German
        <i>
         sparen
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Catalan_influence">
        Catalan influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Catalan influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Following the
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Vespers" title="Sicilian Vespers">
        Sicilian Vespers
       </a>
       of 1282, the kingdom was to come under the influence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">
        Kingdom of Aragon
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and so the
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan language
       </a>
       (and the closely related
       <a href="/wiki/Aragonese_language" title="Aragonese language">
        Aragonese
       </a>
       ) would add a new layer of vocabulary in the succeeding century. For the whole of the 14th century, both Catalan and Sicilian were the official languages of the royal court.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sicilian was also used to record the proceedings of the
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">
        parliament
       </a>
       of Sicily (one of the oldest parliaments in Europe) and for other official purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla153_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla153-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While it is often difficult to determine whether a word has come to us directly from Catalan (as opposed to Provençal or Spanish), the following are likely to be such examples:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         addunarisi
        </i>
        – to notice, realise (from
        <i>
         adonar-se
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         affruntarisi
        </i>
        – to be embarrassed (from
        <i>
         afrontar-se
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         arruciari
        </i>
        – to moisten, soak (from
        <i>
         arruixar
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         criscimonia
        </i>
        – growth, development (from
        <i>
         creiximoni
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         muccaturi
        </i>
        – handkerchief (from
        <i>
         mocador
        </i>
        ; but French
        <i>
         muchoir
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         priàrisi
        </i>
        – to be pleased (from
        <i>
         prear-se
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         taliàri
        </i>
        – to look at somebody/something. (from
        <i>
         talaiar
        </i>
        ; but Arab
        <i>
         tali'a
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_period_to_the_modern_age">
        Spanish period to the modern age
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Spanish period to the modern age">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       By the time the crowns of Castille and Aragon were united in the late 15th century, the Italianisation of written Sicilian in the parliamentary and court records had commenced. By 1543 this process was virtually complete, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
        Tuscan dialect
       </a>
       of Italian becoming the
       <i>
        lingua franca
       </i>
       of the Italian peninsula and supplanting written Sicilian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla153_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla153-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Spanish rule had hastened this process in two important ways:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Unlike the Aragonese, almost immediately the Spanish placed
        <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">
         viceroys
        </a>
        on the Sicilian throne. In a sense, the diminishing prestige of the Sicilian kingdom reflected the decline of Sicilian from an official, written language to eventually a spoken language amongst predominantly an illiterate population.
       </li>
       <li>
        The expulsion of all
        <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
         Jews
        </a>
        from Spanish dominions ca. 1492 altered the population of Sicily. Not only did the population decline, many of whom were involved in important educated industries, but some of these Jewish families had been in Sicily for around 1,500 years, and Sicilian was their native language which they used in their schools. Thus the seeds of a possible broad-based education system utilising books written in Sicilian was lost.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla153_58-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla153-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Spanish rule lasted over three centuries (not counting the Aragonese and
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Bourbon" title="House of Bourbon">
        Bourbon
       </a>
       periods on either side) and had a significant influence on the Sicilian vocabulary. The following words are of Spanish derivation:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         arricugghirisi
        </i>
        – to return home; (from
        <i>
         recogerse
        </i>
        ; but Catalan
        <i>
         recollir-se
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         balanza
        </i>
        – scales (from
        <i>
         balanza
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         fileccia
        </i>
        – arrow (from
        <i>
         flecha
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         làstima
        </i>
        – lament, annoyance (from
        <i>
         lástima
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         pinzèddu
        </i>
        – brush (from
        <i>
         pincel
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-56">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         ricivu
        </i>
        – receipt (from
        <i>
         recibo
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         spagnari
        </i>
        – to be frightened ( cross over of Sic.
        <i>
         appagnari
        </i>
        with Sp.
        <i>
         espantarse
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         spatari
        </i>
        – to impede or disarm someone of his sword (Sic.
        <i>
         spata
        </i>
        and Sp.
        <i>
         espadar
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         sulità
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         sulitati
        </i>
        – solitude (from
        <i>
         soledad
        </i>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giarizzo_45-60">
         <a href="#cite_note-Giarizzo-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
        }
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Since the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Unification" title="Italian Unification">
        Italian Unification
       </a>
       (the
       <i>
        Risorgimento
       </i>
       of 1860–1861), the Sicilian language has been significantly influenced by (Tuscan) Italian. During the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascist" title="Fascist">
        Fascist
       </a>
       period it became obligatory that Italian be taught and spoken in all schools, whereas up to that point, Sicilian had been used extensively in schools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla163_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla163-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This process has quickened since
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       due to improving educational standards and the impact of mass media, such that increasingly, even within the family home, Sicilian is not necessarily the language of choice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla163_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla163-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Regional_Assembly" title="Sicilian Regional Assembly">
        Sicilian Regional Assembly
       </a>
       voted to make the teaching of Sicilian a part of the school curriculum at primary school level, but as of 2007 only a fraction of schools teach Sicilian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cipolla163_59-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cipolla163-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is also little in the way of mass media offered in Sicilian. The combination of these factors means that the Sicilian language continues to adopt Italian vocabulary and grammatical forms to such an extent that many Sicilians themselves cannot distinguish between correct and incorrect Sicilian language usage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Distinguishing_features_of_Sicilian">
        Distinguishing features of Sicilian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Distinguishing features of Sicilian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristic_sounds">
        Characteristic sounds
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Characteristic sounds">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sicilian has a number of consonant sounds that, although not unique to Sicilian, certainly set it apart from the other major Romance languages. The most unusual sounds include, but are not limited to, the
       <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
        retroflex consonants
       </a>
       or cacuminals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CipollaDD_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CipollaDD-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner11_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bonner11-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        ḌḌ— The
        <i>
         -ll-
        </i>
        sound (in words of Latin origin, for example) manifests itself in Sicilian as a
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_stop" title="Voiced retroflex stop">
         voiced retroflex stop
        </a>
        with the tip of the tongue curled up and back, a sound rare in the Romance languages. Traditionally in Sicilian Latin, this sound was written as ÐÐ, and in more contemporary usage
        <i>
         -dd-
        </i>
        has been used
        <i>
         ,
        </i>
        also often found written
        <i>
         ddh
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         ddr
        </i>
        . In the
        <a href="/wiki/Cad%C3%A8mia_Siciliana" title="Cadèmia Siciliana">
         Cadèmia Siciliana
        </a>
        orthographical proposal as well as the
        <a href="/wiki/Vocabolario_siciliano" title="Vocabolario siciliano">
         Vocabulario Siciliano
        </a>
        descriptive orthography the letter ḍḍ is used.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cademia_65-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cademia-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The sound itself is not
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiced dental and alveolar stops">
          d
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        but rather
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_stop" title="Voiced retroflex stop">
          ɖ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        . For example, the Italian word
        <i>
         bello
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">
          [ˈbɛllo]
         </a>
        </span>
        is
        <i>
         beḍḍu
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner11_64-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Bonner11-64">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          [ˈbɛɖʊ]
         </a>
        </span>
        in Sicilian. This sound
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɖ]
        </span>
        also evolved from Latin
        <i>
         -ll-
        </i>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
         Sardinian
        </a>
        , to an extent in
        <a href="/wiki/Asturian_language" title="Asturian language">
         Asturian
        </a>
        , elsewhere in Southern Italy, and in many northwestern Tuscan dialects.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         DR
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         TR
        </i>
        — The Sicilian pronunciation of the
        <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
         digraphs
        </a>
        <i>
         -dr-
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         -tr-
        </i>
        as [ɖ:ɾ] and [ʈɾ].
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cademia_65-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cademia-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         RR
        </i>
        — The
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">
         consonant cluster
        </a>
        <i>
         -rr-
        </i>
        , depending on the variety of Sicilian, can be a strongly trilled [ɾ:]
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cademia_65-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cademia-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        or a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_sibilant" title="Voiced retroflex sibilant">
         voiced retroflex sibilant
        </a>
        (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiced retroflex fricative">
          ʐ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        according to
        <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
         IPA
        </a>
        notation).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner11_64-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Bonner11-64">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        At the beginning of a word, the single letter
        <i>
         -r-
        </i>
        is similarly always pronounced double, though this is not indicated orthographically. This phenomenon, however, does not include words that include an 'r' resulting from
        <a href="/wiki/Rhotacism_(sound_change)" title="Rhotacism (sound change)">
         rhotacism
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         renti
        </i>
        from
        <i>
         denti
        </i>
        ) or
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
         assimilation
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         ranni
        </i>
        from
        <i>
         granni
        </i>
        ). This innovation is also found under slightly different circumstances in
        <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">
         Polish
        </a>
        , where it is spelled
        <i>
         rz
        </i>
        , and in some Northern Norwegian dialects, where speaker vary between
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʐ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_non-sibilant_fricative" title="Voiced alveolar non-sibilant fricative">
          ɹ̝
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         STR
        </i>
        — The
        <a href="/wiki/Digraphs_and_trigraphs" title="Digraphs and trigraphs">
         trigraph
        </a>
        <i>
         -str-
        </i>
        in Sicilian is [ʂ:ɾ]
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cademia_65-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cademia-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiceless retroflex fricative">
          ʂː
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ). The
        <i>
         t
        </i>
        is not pronounced at all and there is a faint whistle between the
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        and the
        <i>
         r
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CipollaDD_63-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-CipollaDD-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        An example of this trigraph is the
        <i>
         shr
        </i>
        sound heard in English
        <i>
         shred
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CipollaDD_63-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-CipollaDD-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Latin FL
        </i>
        — The other unique Sicilian sound is found in those words that have been derived from Latin words containing
        <i>
         -fl-
        </i>
        . In standard literary Sicilian, the sound is rendered as
        <i>
         ci
        </i>
        (representing the
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless palatal fricative">
         voiceless palatal fricative
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_fricative#Palatal" title="Voiceless palatal fricative">
          ç
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ), e.g.
        <i>
         ciumi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈçuːmɪ]
        </span>
        , but can also be found in written form as
        <i>
         hi
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sci
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         x
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         çi
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CipollaDD_63-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-CipollaDD-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filologici_e_Linguistici_Siciliani_2002_67-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Filologici_e_Linguistici_Siciliani_2002-67">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_vowel_system" title="Sicilian vowel system">
         Sicilian vowel system
        </a>
        . Unlike the seven vowels of
        <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
         Vulgar Latin
        </a>
        and many modern Romance languages, the Sicilian vowel system only includes five:
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
          a
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         è
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
          ɛ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">
          i
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         ò
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
          ɔ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">
          u
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CipollaDD_63-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-CipollaDD-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This results in the unstressed vowel
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        of Latin becoming an unstressed
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">
          ʊ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in Sicilian.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull24_68-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull24-68">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This causes the vowel
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        to have a far greater presence than the vowel
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        in Sicilian, whereas the opposite is true in other Romance languages such as Spanish and Italian (notwithstanding the conservative nature of Sicilian, which retains the vowel
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        of the Latin stems
        <i>
         -us
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         -um
        </i>
        ). Likewise, the unstressed vowel
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        of Latin becomes unstressed vowel
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">
          ɪ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        in Sicilian. As a result, the vowel
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        has a much greater presence than vowel
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        in Sicilian.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hull24_68-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hull24-68">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In addition, one will never find a Sicilian word ending in the unaccented vowels
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        , with the exception of monosyllabic conjunctions. Due to the influence of Italian in the media after
        <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
         World War II
        </a>
        , as well as the recent influx of English terminology related to technology and globalization, there is an increasing number of words entering the Sicilian lexicon that do not adhere to the Sicilian vowel system.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Consonantal lenition
        </i>
        — A further range of consonantal sound shifts occurred between the Vulgar Latin introduced to the island following Norman rule and the subsequent development of the Sicilian language. These sound shifts include: Latin
        <i>
         -nd-
        </i>
        to Sicilian
        <i>
         -nn-
        </i>
        ; Latin
        <i>
         -mb-
        </i>
        to Sicilian
        <i>
         -mm-
        </i>
        ; Latin
        <i>
         -pl-
        </i>
        to Sicilian
        <i>
         -chi-
        </i>
        ; and Latin
        <i>
         -li-
        </i>
        to Sicilian
        <i>
         -gghi-
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
         <a href="#cite_note-69">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Rhotacism —
        </i>
        This transformation is characterized by the substitution of d by r. In Sicilian this is produced by a single flap of the tongue against the upper alveolar ridge, and this actually sounds like a kind of d sound. This phenomenon is known as rhotacism, that is, the substitution of r for another consonant, and it is commonly found both in Eastern Sicilian and Western Sicilian. It can occur internally, or it can affect initial d. Examples :
        <i>
         Pedi
        </i>
        (foot) is pronounced
        <i>
         -peri-
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         Madonna
        </i>
        ( The Virgin Mary) is pronounced
        <i>
         -Maronna-; Diri
        </i>
        (to say) is pronounced
        <i>
         -riri-
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
         <a href="#cite_note-70">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This is found elsewhere in Southern Italy, especially in Neapolitan.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gemination_and_contractions">
        Gemination and contractions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Gemination and contractions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Rarely indicated in writing, spoken Sicilian exhibits
       <a href="/wiki/Syntactic_gemination" title="Syntactic gemination">
        syntactic gemination
       </a>
       or
       <i>
        dubbramentu
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which means that the first consonant of a word is lengthened when it is preceded by a vowel in the preceding word, e.g.
       <i>
        è bonu
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌɛbˈbɔːnʊ]
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The letter
       <i>
        j
       </i>
       at the start of a word can have three separate sounds, depending on what precedes the word.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECipolla2005_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECipolla2005-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For instance, in
       <i>
        jornu
       </i>
       (day), the
       <i>
        j
       </i>
       is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       as in English
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈjɔɾnʊ]
       </span>
       . However, after a
       <a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">
        nasal consonant
       </a>
       , it is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɡ]
       </span>
       as in
       <i>
        un jornu
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [uŋˈɡɔɾnʊ]
       </span>
       .
       <i>
        Tri jorna
       </i>
       (three days) is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌʈ͡ʂiɡˈɡjɔɾna]
       </span>
       , the
       <i>
        j
       </i>
       becoming
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_stop#Palatal_or_alveolo-palatal" title="Voiced palatal stop">
         ɡj
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (like English
       <i>
        gu
       </i>
       in "argue"), after a vowel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another difference between the written and spoken languages is the extent to which
       <a href="/wiki/Contraction_(grammar)" title="Contraction (grammar)">
        contractions
       </a>
       will occur in everyday speech. Thus a common expression such as
       <i>
        avemu a accattari...
       </i>
       (we have to go and buy...) will generally be reduced to
       <i>
        amâ 'ccattari
       </i>
       when talking to family and friends.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._56_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._56-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">
        circumflex
       </a>
       is commonly used in denoting a wide range of contractions in the written language, in particular, the joining of simple prepositions and the definite article. Examples:
       <i>
        di lu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        dû
       </i>
       (of the),
       <i>
        a lu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        ô
       </i>
       (to the),
       <i>
        pi lu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        pû
       </i>
       (for the),
       <i>
        nta lu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        ntô
       </i>
       (in the), etc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_the_formation_of_plurals">
        Gender and the formation of plurals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gender and the formation of plurals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Generally speaking, Sicilian has the same ending for feminine nouns (and their adjectives) as most Romance languages, that being the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       , for example:
       <i>
        casa
       </i>
       (house),
       <i>
        porta
       </i>
       (door),
       <i>
        carta
       </i>
       (paper), but there are exceptions to this rule, for example,
       <i>
        soru
       </i>
       (sister),
       <i>
        ficu
       </i>
       (fig). The ending for masculine nouns is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /u/
       </span>
       , for example:
       <i>
        omu
       </i>
       (man),
       <i>
        libbru
       </i>
       (book),
       <i>
        nomu
       </i>
       (name). The ending
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       can be either masculine or feminine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._25_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._25-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike standard Italian, Sicilian uses one letter,
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       , to denote the plural for both masculine and feminine nouns, for example:
       <i>
        casi
       </i>
       (houses),
       <i>
        porti
       </i>
       (doors),
       <i>
        tàuli
       </i>
       (tables). There are also many exceptions to this rule which are not always shared by Italian, for example:
       <i>
        libbra
       </i>
       (books),
       <i>
        jorna
       </i>
       (days),
       <i>
        jòcura
       </i>
       (games),
       <i>
        vrazza
       </i>
       (arms),
       <i>
        jardìna
       </i>
       (gardens),
       <i>
        scrittura
       </i>
       (writers),
       <i>
        signa
       </i>
       (signs), etc.,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._25_78-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._25-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the following three common nouns are invariable in the plural:
       <i>
        manu
       </i>
       (hand/hands),
       <i>
        ficu
       </i>
       (fig/figs) and
       <i>
        soru
       </i>
       (sister/sisters).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Omission_of_initial_Latin_.22i.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Omission_of_initial_Latin_"i"'>
        Omission of initial Latin "i"
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title='Edit section: Omission of initial Latin "i"'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the vast majority of instances where the originating Latin word has had an initial "i", the Sicilian has dropped it completely. This can also happen occasionally where there was once an initial "e", and to a lesser extent "a" and "o". Examples:
       <i>
        mpurtanti
       </i>
       "important",
       <i>
        gnuranti
       </i>
       "ignorant",
       <i>
        nimicu
       </i>
       "enemy",
       <i>
        ntirissanti
       </i>
       "interesting",
       <i>
        llustrari
       </i>
       "to illustrate",
       <i>
        mmàggini
       </i>
       "image",
       <i>
        cona
       </i>
       "icon",
       <i>
        Miricanu
       </i>
       "American".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filologici_e_Linguistici_Siciliani_2002_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Filologici_e_Linguistici_Siciliani_2002-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Verb_.22to_have.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Verb_"to_have"'>
        Verb "to have"
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title='Edit section: Verb "to have"'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sicilian only has one
       <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">
        auxiliary verb
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        aviri
       </i>
       "to have".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camilleri488_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Camilleri488-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Aviri
       </i>
       is also used to denote obligation (e.g.
       <i>
        avi a jiri
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌaːvjaɡˈɡiːɾi]
       </span>
       '[he/she] has to go').
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._56_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bonner,_J_K_2001_p._56-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is also used to form the future tense, as Sicilian, for the most part, no longer has a synthetic future tense. For example:
       <i>
        avi a cantari
       </i>
       '[he/she] will sing' (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌaːvjakkanˈtaːɾɪ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌaːwakkanˈdaːɾɪ]
       </span>
       , depending on the dialect).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camilleri488_81-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Camilleri488-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Verb_.22to_go.22_and_the_periphrastic_future">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Verb_"to_go"_and_the_periphrastic_future'>
        Verb "to go" and the periphrastic future
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title='Edit section: Verb "to go" and the periphrastic future'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As in English, and most Romance languages, Sicilian may use the verb
       <i>
        jiri
       </i>
       , to go, to signify the act of being about to do something.
       <i>
        Vaiu a cantari
       </i>
       (pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˌvaːjwakkanˈtaːɾɪ]
       </span>
       ) "I'm going to sing", literally "I go to sing." In this way,
       <i>
        jiri
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       + infinitive can also be a way to form the simple future construction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tenses_and_moods">
        Tenses and moods
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Tenses and moods">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The main conjugations in Sicilian are illustrated below with the verb
       <i>
        èssiri
       </i>
       , "to be".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Infinitive
        </th>
        <td colspan="6">
         èssiri / siri
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Gerund
        </th>
        <td colspan="6">
         essennu / sennu
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Past participle
        </th>
        <td colspan="6">
         statu
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Indicative
        </th>
        <th>
         eu/iu/ju
        </th>
        <th>
         tu
        </th>
        <th>
         iḍḍu
        </th>
        <th>
         nuàutri
        </th>
        <th>
         vuàutri
        </th>
        <th>
         iḍḍi
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Present
        </th>
        <td>
         sugnu
        </td>
        <td>
         si'
        </td>
        <td>
         esti / e'
        </td>
        <td>
         semu
        </td>
        <td>
         siti
        </td>
        <td>
         sunnu / su'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Imperfect
        </th>
        <td>
         era
        </td>
        <td>
         eri
        </td>
        <td>
         era
        </td>
        <td>
         èramu
        </td>
        <td>
         èravu
        </td>
        <td>
         èranu
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Preterite
        </th>
        <td>
         fui
        </td>
        <td>
         fusti
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>
         fomu
        </td>

         fùstivu
        </td>

         <sup>

         </sup>

        <td>

        </td>

       <tr>
        <th>
         Subjunctive
        </th>

         ju
        </th>
        <th>
         tu
        </th>
        <th>
         iḍḍu
        </th>
        <th>
         nuàutri
        </th>
        <th>
         vuàutri
        </th>
        <th>
         iḍḍi
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Present
        </th>
        <td>
         sia
        </td>

         si' / fussi
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         siamu
        </td>
        <td>
         siati
        </td>
        <td>
         sianu
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Imperfect
        </th>
        <td>
         fussi
        </td>
        <td>
         fussi
        </td>

        </td>
        <td>
         fùssimu
        </td>
        <td>
         fùssivu
        </td>
        <td>
         fùssiru
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Imperative
        </th>
        <th>
         —
        </th>
        <th>
         tu
        </th>
        <th>
         vassìa
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         —
        </th>
        <th>
         vuàutri
        </th>
        <th>
         —
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <td>
         —
        </td>
        <td>
         sì
        </td>
        <td>
         fussi
        </td>
        <td>
         —
        </td>
        <td>
         siti
        </td>
        <td>
         —
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       1. The synthetic future is rarely used, and as Camilleri explains, continues its decline towards complete disuse;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camilleri488_81-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Camilleri488-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       instead, the following methods are used to express the future:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        use of the present indicative, usually preceded by an adverb of time:
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           Stasira vaiu ô tiatru
          </i>
          — "This evening I go to the theatre"; or, using a similar English construction, "This evening I am going to the theatre"
         </dd>
         <dd>
          <i>
           Dumani ti scrivu
          </i>
          — "Tomorrow I [will] write to you."
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        use of a compound form consisting of the appropriate conjugation of
        <i>
         aviri a
        </i>
        ("have to") in combination with the infinitive form of the verb in question:
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           Stasira haju a gghìri/ìri ô tiatru
          </i>
          — "This evening I will [/must] go to the theatre."
         </dd>
         <dd>
          <i>
           Dumani t'haju a scrìviri
          </i>
          — "Tomorrow I will [/must] write to you."
         </dd>
         <dd>
          In speech, the contracted forms of
          <i>
           aviri
          </i>
          often come into play:
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             haju a
            </i>
            →
            <i>
             /hâ/hê
            </i>
            ;
            <i>
             hai a
            </i>
            →
            <i>
             hâ
            </i>
            ,
            <i>
             havi a
            </i>
            →
            <i>
             havâ
            </i>
            ,
            <i>
             avemu a
            </i>
            →
            <i>
             hamâ
            </i>
            ;
            <i>
             aviti a
            </i>
            →
            <i>
             hatâ
            </i>
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Dumani t'hâ scrìviri
            </i>
            — "Tomorrow I will [/must] write to you".
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
             <a href="#cite_note-85">
              [85]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       2. The synthetic conditional has also fallen into disuse (except for the dialect spoken
       <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">
        Messina
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        missinisi
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The conditional has two tenses:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        1) The present conditional, which is replaced by either:
        <dl>
         <dd>
          i) the present indicative:
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Cci chiamu si tu mi duni lu sò nùmmaru
            </i>
            — "I [would] call her if you [would] give me her number", or
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </dd>
         <dd>
          ii) the imperfect subjunctive:
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Cci chiamassi si tu mi dassi lu sò nùmmaru
            </i>
            — "I'd call her if you would give me her number"; and
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        2) the past conditional, which is replaced by the pluperfect subjunctive:
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Cci avissi jutu si tu m'avissi dittu [/diciutu] unni esti / e
            </i>
            — "I'd have gone if you would have told me where it is"
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Note that in a hypothetical statement, both tenses are replaced by the imperfect and pluperfect subjunctive:
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <dl>
           <dd>
            <i>
             Si fussi riccu m'accattassi nu palazzu
            </i>
            — "If I were rich I would buy a palace"
           </dd>
           <dd>
            <i>
             S'avissi travagghiatu nun avissi patutu la misèria
            </i>
            — "If I had worked I wouldn't have suffered the misery".
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
             <a href="#cite_note-87">
              [87]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </dd>
          </dl>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       3. The 2nd-person singular (polite) utilises the older form of the present subjunctive, for example
       <i>
        parrassi
       </i>
       , which has the effect of softening it somewhat into a request rather than an instruction. The 2nd-person singular and plural forms of the imperative are identical to the present indicative, with the exception of the 2nd-person singular -ari verbs, where the ending is the same as for the 3rd person singular, for example
       <i>
        parra
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_the_written_language">
        Examples of the written language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Examples of the written language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Extracts from three of Sicily's more celebrated poets are offered below to illustrate the written form of Sicilian over the last few centuries:
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Veneziano_(poet)" title="Antonio Veneziano (poet)">
        Antonio Veneziano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Meli" title="Giovanni Meli">
        Giovanni Meli
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nino_Martoglio" title="Nino Martoglio">
        Nino Martoglio
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A translation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">
        Lord's Prayer
       </a>
       can also be found in J K Bonner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is written with three variations: a standard literary form from the island of Sicily and a southern
       <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">
        Apulian
       </a>
       literary form.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extract_from_Antonio_Veneziano">
        Extract from Antonio Veneziano
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Extract from Antonio Veneziano">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="Celia.2C_Lib._2">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Celia,_Lib._2">
        <i>
         Celia, Lib. 2
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Celia, Lib. 2">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       (ca. 1575–1580)
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Sicilian
        </th>
        <th>
         Italian
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Non è xhiamma ordinaria, no, la mia,
        </td>
        <td>
         No, la mia non è fiamma ordinaria,
        </td>
        <td>
         No, mine is no ordinary flame,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         è xhiamma chi sul'iu tegnu e rizettu,
        </td>
        <td>
         è una fiamma che sol'io possiedo e controllo,
        </td>
        <td>
         it's a flame that only I possess and control,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         xhiamma pura e celesti, ch'ardi 'n mia;
        </td>
        <td>
         una fiamma pura e celeste che dientro di me cresce;
        </td>
        <td>
         a pure celestial flame that in me grows;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         per gran misteriu e cu stupendu effettu.
        </td>
        <td>
         da un grande mistero e con stupendo effetto.
        </td>
        <td>
         by a great mystery and with great effect.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Amuri, 'ntentu a fari idulatria,
        </td>
        <td>
         l'Amore, desiderante d'adorare icone,
        </td>
        <td>
         Love, wanting to worship idols,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         s'ha novamenti sazerdoti elettu;
        </td>
        <td>
         è diventato sacerdote un'altra volta;
        </td>
        <td>
         has once again become a high priest;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         tu, sculpita 'ntra st'alma, sìa la dia;
        </td>
        <td>
         tu, scolpita dentro quest'anima, sei la dea;
        </td>
        <td>
         you, sculpted in this soul, are the goddess;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         sacrifiziu lu cori, ara stu pettu.
        </td>
        <td>
         il mio cuore è la vittima, il mio seno è l'altare.
        </td>
        <td>
         my heart is the victim, my breast is the altar.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       (sourced directly from
       <i>
        Arba Sicula
       </i>
       Volume II, 1980)
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extract_from_Giovanni_Meli">
        Extract from Giovanni Meli
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Extract from Giovanni Meli">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="Don_Chisciotti_e_Sanciu_Panza_.28Cantu_quintu.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Don_Chisciotti_e_Sanciu_Panza_(Cantu_quintu)">
        <i>
         Don Chisciotti e Sanciu Panza
        </i>
        (Cantu quintu)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Don Chisciotti e Sanciu Panza (Cantu quintu)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       (~1790)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Sicilian
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Stracanciatu di notti soli jiri;
        </td>
        <td>
         Disguised he roams at night alone;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         S'ammuccia ntra purtuni e cantuneri;
        </td>
        <td>
         Hiding in any nook and cranny;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         cu vacabunni ci mustra piaciri;
        </td>
        <td>
         he enjoys the company of vagabonds;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         poi lu so sbiu sunnu li sumeri,
        </td>
        <td>
         however, donkeys are his real diversion,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         li pruteggi e li pigghia a ben vuliri,
        </td>
        <td>
         he protects them and looks after all their needs,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         li tratta pri parenti e amici veri;
        </td>
        <td>
         treating them as real family and friends;
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         siccomu ancora è n'amicu viraci
        </td>
        <td>
         since he remains a true friend
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         di li bizzarri, capricciusi e audaci.
        </td>
        <td>
         of all who are bizarre, capricious and bold.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeli1995_91-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeli1995-91">
           [91]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extract_from_Nino_Martoglio">
        Extract from Nino Martoglio
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Extract from Nino Martoglio">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span id="Briscula_.27n_Cumpagni">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Briscula_'n_Cumpagni">
        <i>
         Briscula 'n Cumpagni
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Briscula 'n Cumpagni">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       (~1900; trans: A game of
       <i>
        Briscula
       </i>
       amongst friends)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Sicilian
        </th>
        <th>
         Italian
        </th>
        <th>
         English
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         — Càrricu, mancu? Cca cc'è 'n sei di spati!...
        </td>
        <td>
         — Nemmeno un carico? Qui c'è un sei di spade!...
        </td>
        <td>
         — A high card perhaps? Here's the six of spades!...
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         — E chi schifiu è, di sta manera?
        </td>
        <td>
         — Ma che schifo, in questo modo?
        </td>
        <td>
         — What is this rubbish you're playing?
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Don Peppi Nnappa, d'accussì jucati?
        </td>
        <td>
         Signor Peppe Nappa,
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
          <a href="#cite_note-93">
           [93]
          </a>
         </sup>
         ma giocate così?
        </td>
        <td>
         Who taught you to play this game?
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         — Massari e scecchi tutta 'a tistera,
        </td>
        <td>
         — Messere e asino con tutti i finimenti,
        </td>
        <td>
         — My dear gentlemen and donkeys with all your finery,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         comu vi l'haju a diri, a vastunati,
        </td>
        <td>
         come ve lo devo dire, forse a bastonate,
        </td>
        <td>
         as I have repeatedly told you till I'm blue in the face,
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         ca mancu haju sali di salera!
        </td>
        <td>
         che non ho nemmeno il sale per la saliera!
        </td>
        <td>
         I ain't got nothing that's even worth a pinch a salt!
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Influences_on_the_Italian_language">
        Influences on the Italian language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Influences on the Italian language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="1255" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg/220px-Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg/330px-Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg/440px-Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minchia_graffiti_in_Turin_January_2017.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Minchia
         </i>
         : graffiti in
         <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">
          Turin
         </a>
         , January 2017
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As one of the most-spoken languages of Italy, Sicilian has notably influenced the Italian lexicon. In fact, there are several Sicilian words that are nowadays part of the Italian language; they usually refer to things closely associated to Sicilian culture, with some notable exceptions:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Arancini" title="Arancini">
          arancino
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         arancinu
        </i>
        ): arancino, a Sicilian cuisine specialty;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Canestrato" title="Canestrato">
          canestrato
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         ncannistratu
        </i>
        ): a cheese typical of Sicily;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cannoli" title="Cannoli">
          cannolo
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         cannolu
        </i>
        ): cannolo, a Sicilian pastry;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         cannolicchio
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         cannulicchiu
        </i>
        ): razor-clam;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         carnezzeria
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         carnizzaria
        </i>
        ): butcher's shop;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         caruso
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         carusu
        </i>
        ): boy;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cassata" title="Cassata">
          cassata
         </a>
        </i>
        : cassata, a Sicilian pastry;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cirneco_dell%27Etna" title="Cirneco dell'Etna">
          cirneco
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         cirnecu
        </i>
        ): a small breed of dogs common in Sicily;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cosca" title="Cosca">
          cosca
         </a>
        </i>
        : a small group of criminals affiliated to the Sicilian mafia;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         curatolo
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         curatulu
        </i>
        ): watchman in a farm, with a yearly contract;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         dammuso
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         dammusu
        </i>
        ): stony habitation typical of the island of
        <a href="/wiki/Pantelleria" title="Pantelleria">
         Pantelleria
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         intrallazzo
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         ntrallazzu
        </i>
        ): illegal exchange of goods or favours, but in a wider sense also cheat, intrigue;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Jew%27s_harp" title="Jew's harp">
          marranzano
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         marranzanu
        </i>
        ):
        <a href="/wiki/Jew%27s_harp" title="Jew's harp">
         Jew's harp
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         marrobbio
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         marrubbiu
        </i>
        ): quick variation of sea level produced by a store of water in the coasts as a consequence of either wind action or an atmospheric depression;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         minchia
        </i>
        : penis in its original meaning, but also stupid person, is also widely used as interjection to show either astonishment or rage;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         picciotto
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         picciottu
        </i>
        ): young man, but also the lowest grade in the Mafia hierarchy;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Pizzino" title="Pizzino">
          pizzino
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         pizzinu
        </i>
        ): small piece of paper;
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Pizzo_(extortion)" title="Pizzo (extortion)">
          pizzo
         </a>
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         pizzu
        </i>
        ): literally meaning beak in Sicilian, it is protection money paid to the Mafia; it comes from the saying
        <i>
         fari vagnari a pizzu
        </i>
        (to wet one's beak).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         quaquaraquà
        </i>
        : person devoid of value, nonentity; (onomatopoeia?; "the duck wants a say")
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         scasare
        </i>
        (from
        <i>
         scasari
        </i>
        ): to leave en masse (means literally to move home);
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         stidda
        </i>
        (it.:
        <i>
         stella
        </i>
        ): lower Mafia organization.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_situation_today">
        Language situation today
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Language situation today">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sicily">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sicilian is estimated to have 5,000,000 speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it remains very much a home language spoken among peers and close associates.
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian" title="Regional Italian">
        Regional Italian
       </a>
       has encroached on Sicilian, most evidently in the speech of the younger generations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruffino108_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ruffino108-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In terms of the written language, in Sicily it is mainly restricted to poetry and theatre.
      </p>
      <p>
       The education system does not support the language, despite recent legislative changes, as mentioned previously. Local universities do not carry courses in Sicilian, or where they do it is described as
       <i>
        dialettologia
       </i>
       , that is, the study of dialects.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora">
        Diaspora
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Diaspora">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Outside Sicily, there is an extensive Sicilian diaspora living in several major cities across South and North America, as well as other parts of Europe and Australia, where Sicilian has been preserved to varying degrees.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Sicilian-American organization
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Arba_Sicula" title="Arba Sicula">
         Arba Sicula
        </a>
       </i>
       publishes stories, poems and essays, in Sicilian with English translations, in an effort to preserve the Sicilian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Media">
        Media
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sicilian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Media">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The movie
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_Terra_Trema" title="La Terra Trema">
         La Terra Trema
        </a>
       </i>
       (1948) is in Sicilian, using many local, non-professional actors.
      </p>

              </a>
